Since live anthology drama series of the mid-1950s were usually hour-long shows, the title highlighted the network's intention to present something unusual, a weekly series of hour-and-a-half dramas rather than 60-minute plays. Playhouse 90 began as a pitch by Frank Stanton\u2014the formidable, forward-thinking right-hand man to CBS chairman William S. Paley\u2014during a brainstorming session for program ideas. The project was ultimately developed by Hubbell Robinson, a CBS vice president who received no screen credit on Playhouse 90 but is often described as its creator.", "poster_path": "/6srGbszH8amK1JiJRS2YzRcoDVw.jpg", "media_type": "tv", "adult": false, "original_language": "en", "genre_ids": [18], "popularity": 5.9753, "first_air_date": "1956-10-04", "vote_average": 7.5, "vote_count": 6, "origin_country": ["US"]}, {"backdrop_path": null, "id": 11477, "name": "Alcoa Theatre", "original_name": "Alcoa Theatre", "overview": "Alcoa Theatre is a half-hour American anthology series telecast on NBC at 9:30 pm on alternate Monday nights from October 7, 1957 to September 16, 1960. Alcoa Theatre was syndicated together with Goodyear Theatre as Award Theatre.\n\nIn 1955, The Alcoa Hour premiered in a one-hour format aired on Sunday nights, but it was reduced to 30 minutes, retitled Alcoa Theatre, and moved to Monday evening in 1957.
